                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2014 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-05-28"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The colonization of land by plants was a key event in the evolution of life. Here we report the draft genome sequence of the filamentous terrestrial alga Klebsormidium flaccidum (Division Charophyta, Order Klebsormidiales) to elucidate the early transition step from aquatic algae to land plants. Comparison of the genome sequence with that of other algae and land plants demonstrate that K. flaccidum acquired many genes specific to land plants. We demonstrate that K. flaccidum indeed produces several plant hormones and homologues of some of the signalling intermediates required for hormone actions in higher plants. The K. flaccidum genome also encodes a primitive system to protect against the harmful effects of high-intensity light. The presence of these plant-related systems in K. flaccidum suggests that, during evolution, this alga acquired the fundamental machinery required for adaptation to terrestrial environments. Plant colonization of land is an important evolutionary event. Here, the authors sequence the genome of a filamentous terrestrial alga and, through a comparative analysis with related algae and land plant species, provide insight into how aquatic algae adapted to terrestrial environments.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The colonization of land by plants was a key event in the evolution of life. Here we report the draft genome sequence of the filamentous terrestrial alga <i>Klebsormidium flaccidum</i> (Division Charophyta, Order Klebsormidiales) to elucidate the early transition step from aquatic algae to land plants. Comparison of the genome sequence with that of other algae and land plants demonstrate that <i>K. flaccidum</i> acquired many genes specific to land plants. We demonstrate that <i>K. flaccidum</i> indeed produces several plant hormones and homologues of some of the signalling intermediates required for hormone actions in higher plants. The <i>K. flaccidum</i> genome also encodes a primitive system to protect against the harmful effects of high-intensity light. The presence of these plant-related systems in <i>K. flaccidum</i> suggests that, during evolution, this alga acquired the fundamental machinery required for adaptation to terrestrial environments.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The colonization of land by plants was a key event in the evolution of life, making the modern terrestrial environment habitable by supplying various nutrients<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Parnell, J. &amp; Foster, S. Ordovician ash geochemistry and the establishment of land plants. Geochem. Trans. 13, 7 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1151">1</a></sup> and sufficient atmospheric oxygen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Scott, A. C. &amp; Glasspool, I. J. The diversification of Paleozoic fire systems and fluctuations in atmospheric oxygen concentration. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 10861â10865 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1155">2</a></sup>. It is generally accepted that the ancestor(s) of current terrestrial plants was closely related to present-day charophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lewis, L. A. &amp; McCourt, R. M. Green algae and the origin of land plants. Am. J. Bot. 91, 1535â1556 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1159">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1â46 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1162">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Timme, R. E., Bachvaroff, T. R. &amp; Delwiche, C. F. Broad phylogenomic sampling and the sister lineage of land plants. PLoS ONE 7, e29696 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1165">5</a></sup>. However, the fragmentary genome sequence data available for charophytes has frustrated efforts to find evidence consistent with the proposed transition of a charophyte(s) to the first land plants. The colonization of land by plants must have been preceded by the transition of aquatic algae to terrestrial algae. During this process, the transition species of aquatic algae must have acquired a range of adaptive mechanisms to cope with the harsh features of terrestrial environments, such as drought, high-intensity light and UV radiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Rensing, S. A. et al. The Physcomitrella Genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science 319, 64â69 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1169">6</a></sup>. In addition to making these adaptations, land plants needed to simultaneously enlarge their body sizes through cellular differentiation. The primary features that enabled primitive aquatic plants to colonize land have yet to be established. Given that these features must have a genetic basis and that the intermediate genomes of the relatives between aquatic algae and terrestrial plants must lead to clues to these crucial factors, comparative genomic analyses involving charophytic algaeâwhich comprise streptophytes with embryophytes (land plants)âseem critical for elucidation of these features.</p><p>The charophytic algae <i>Klebsormidium</i> usually consist of multicellular and non-branching filaments without differentiated or specialized cells. <i>Klebsormidium</i> species therefore have primitive body plans, and most species that have adapted to land also can survive in fresh water<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1â46 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1182">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rindi, F. et al. Phylogenetic relationships in Interfilum and Klebsormidium (Klebsormidiophyceae, Streptophyta). Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 58, 218â231 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1185">7</a></sup>. In fact, tolerance to typical terrestrial stresses like drought<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Morison, M. O. &amp; Sheath, R. G. Response to desiccation stress by Klebsormidium rivulare (Ulotrichales, Chlorophyta) from a Rhode Island stream. Phycologia 24, 129â145 (1985)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1189">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Elster, J. et al. Freezing and desiccation injury resistance in the filamentous green alga Klebsormidium from the Antarctic, Arctic and Slovakia. Biologia 63, 843â851 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1192">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Karsten, U. &amp; Holzinger, A. Light, temperature, and desiccation effects on photosynthetic activity, and drought-induced ultrastructural changes in the green alga Klebsormidium dissectum (Streptophyta) from a high alpine soil crust. Microb. Ecol. 63, 51â63 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1195">10</a></sup> or freezing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Elster, J. et al. Freezing and desiccation injury resistance in the filamentous green alga Klebsormidium from the Antarctic, Arctic and Slovakia. Biologia 63, 843â851 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1199">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Nagao, M., Matsui, K. &amp; Uemura, M. Klebsormidium flaccidum, a charophycean green alga, exhibits cold acclimation that is closely associated with compatible solute accumulation and ultrastructural changes. Plant Cell Environ. 31, 872â885 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1202">11</a></sup> has been reported in some <i>Klebsormidium</i> species. These features suggest that an ancestor of modern-day members of Klebsormidiales acquired fundamental mechanisms that enable survival in severe land environments that differ substantially from the more stable conditions characteristic of aquatic environments.</p><p>Here we sequence and analyse the genome of the <i>K. flaccidum</i> strain NIES-2285 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Comparison of this genome sequence with available genome sequences of other algae and land plants suggests that <i>K. flaccidum</i> acquired many genes specific to land plants. These include genes essential for plant hormone action and cyclic electron flow (CEF) activityâbiological systems that were probably critical for terrestrialization. Our analysis provides evidence that <i>K. flaccidum</i> has the fundamental machinery required for adaptation to survival in terrestrial environments.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Differential interference microscope image of Klebsormidium flaccidum strain NIES-2285."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Differential interference microscope image of <i>Klebsormidium flaccidum</i> strain NIES-2285.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="515" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><i>K. flaccidum</i> consists of non-branching long filamentous cells. Each cell contains a large chloroplast, which is positioned against the cell wall (parietal chloroplast) and contains a pyrenoid. Arrowhead indicates a pyrenoid surrounded by a few starch grains. Scale bar, 10âÎ¼m.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Total genome size was estimated as 117.1Â±21.8âMb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>), and the DNA and cDNA sequences were determined using both the Roche 454 GS FLX Titanium and Illumina GAIIx platforms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The sequenced DNA reads were assembled into 1,814 scaffolds covering the nuclear (104âMb), plastidic (181âkb) and mitochondrial (106âkb) genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 1</a>). We identified and annotated 16,215 protein-coding genes in the nuclear and organellar genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 1</a>).</p><p>To examine the phylogenetic similarity between <i>K. flaccidum</i>, land plants and other algae, we compared the sequences of 31 highly conserved proteins of 14 species and charophytes (<i>K. flaccidum</i>, 5 land plants, 7 charophytes algae and 9 other algae; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 1</a>). The phylogenetic tree constructed based on the concatenated amino acid sequence alignment of 31 nuclear genes showed that <i>K. flaccidum</i> diverged after <i>Chlorokybus atmophyticus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). This topology was the same as previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lewis, L. A. &amp; McCourt, R. M. Green algae and the origin of land plants. Am. J. Bot. 91, 1535â1556 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1297">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1â46 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1300">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Timme, R. E., Bachvaroff, T. R. &amp; Delwiche, C. F. Broad phylogenomic sampling and the sister lineage of land plants. PLoS ONE 7, e29696 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1303">5</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of 31 genes from 21 species of algae and land plants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Phylogenetic analysis of 31 genes from 21 species of algae and land plants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="458" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The phylogenetic tree was constructed as the optimal maximum-likelihood tree with the concatenation of 31 nuclear-encoded protein and translated ESTs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 1</a>) alignments. Numbers represent support values after 100 bootstrap replicates. The scale bar denotes the number of substitutions per site.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparative analyses for gene families and protein domains</h3><p>We classified all proteins from each of the 15 species whose genome sequences were determined (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 2</a>), revealing that 1,238 proteins of <i>K. flaccidum</i> are shared by land plants, a number greater than that of other algae, although phylogenetic analysis showed that <i>K. flaccidum</i> is an early diverging lineage of charophytes. Hierarchical clustering (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>) based on the presence or absence of homologous genes in individual organisms for 5,447 <i>K. flaccidum</i> gene groups commonly found in other species suggested that the <i>K. flaccidum</i> proteins resemble those of land plants more than those of other algae we analysed. The reciprocal best-hit analysis of conserved proteins of both algae and land plants also supported that <i>K. flaccidum</i> has genetic characters similar to those of land plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparison of proteins among 15 species of algae and land plants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Comparison of proteins among 15 species of algae and land plants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1287" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Numbers of proteins found in both algae and land plants (green), proteins shared among algae (blue), proteins shared among land plants (magenta), and no reciprocal best hit to other species (yellow) with classification via OrthoMCL (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The upper and lower panels represent the number of genes and the percentage, respectively, for the four categories (the genes without counterparts in yellow were excluded for percentage data). (<b>b</b>) Binary heat map of 5,447 gene groups that were identified as non-unique compared with <i>K. flaccidum</i> and the other 14 organisms studied. The columns and rows represent 5,447 groups of <i>K</i>. <i>flaccidum</i> and their counterparts from 14 organisms, respectively. Grey shading indicates that the group in the organism includes at least one gene by OrthoMCL analysis; white indicates no orthologous gene. The coloured bar shows the classification of each <i>K</i>. <i>flaccidum</i> groups as described for <b>a</b>. Dendrogram on the left corresponds to the results of hierarchical clustering for all organisms.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Next, we inferred the history of gene acquisition that enabled terrestrial adaptation by assessing the diversity seen among gene families and protein domains in 15 representative algae and land plants. For this study, paralogues were defined as genes belonging to a gene family containing at least two genes, and singletons were defined as genes lacking any paralogue in each species. The number of gene families was defined as the sum of the gene families of paralogues and singletons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 3</a>). To represent the diversity within the gene complement of each species, we plotted the number of gene families against the total number of genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). For algae, the number of gene families increased proportionally with total gene number. This was not the case, however, for land plants owing to an apparent upper limit of the number of gene families. Compared with the algae analysed, the plants studied contained more paralogous genes in each gene family and fewer singletons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). For <i>K. flaccidum</i>, we found that many paralogues for which the number in land plants was significantly greater were in fact singletons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Notably, these counterpart genes are involved in processes such as cell wall biogenesis, signal transduction, plant hormone-related categories and environmental responses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 2 and 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Gene families and domains in 15 species of algae and land plants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Gene families and domains in 15 species of algae and land plants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="645" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The green filled circle denotes the data point for <i>K. flaccidum</i>, and red and blue circles denote data points for land plants and algae, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 3</a>). (<b>b</b>) Number of domains (open circles) and domain combinations (filled circles) expressed in terms of the total number of genes in each of 15 species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 4</a>). (<b>c</b>) Acquisition in algal genomes of conserved domains (black bars) and domain combinations (white bars) commonly found in land plants. For the land plants analysed (five species), the numbers of conserved domains and domain combinations were 4,894 and 2,801, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 5</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In addition to gene families, we also analysed the number of domains and domain combinations, based on the Pfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D211âD222 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1484">12</a></sup>, in proteins of the 15 species studied. For domain combinations, the numbers, positions and order of domains in each protein were ignored (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 4</a>). For each species, the number of domains and domain combinations were plotted separately against the total number of genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). Although the number of domains in each of <i>K. flaccidum</i>, <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> (moss) and <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> (spike moss) was the maximal value, for angiosperms (flowering plants) the number of domain combinations continued to increase with increasing gene number. Comparison of the total number of Pfam domains in 15 species revealed that 90.7% (4,441/4,894) of the domains and 84.3% (2,360/2,801) of domain combinations that are commonly found in land plants are represented in the <i>K. flaccidum</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 5</a>). Thus, many archetypal genes typically found in modern land plants probably had already been acquired by the ancestor of <i>K. flaccidum</i>. During adaptation to the various challenges associated with terrestrial life, the numbers of these genes increased in land plants because additional paralogues were acquired, thereby providing new combinations of domains as a consequence of gene duplication and shuffling in land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kersting, A. R., Bornberg-Bauer, E., Moore, A. D. &amp; Grath, S. Dynamics and adaptive benefits of protein domain emergence and arrangements during plant genome evolution. Genome Biol. Evol. 4, 316â329 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1516">13</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Streptophyta-specific genes and their roles</h3><p>We next conducted a comprehensive search for systems typically found in land plants that are essential for terrestrial life. The gene ontology categories of the 1,238 Streptophyta-specific genes in <i>K. flaccidum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 2</a>) were assigned based on best hits with respect to <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes/gene families. Several genes are highly enriched in biological process categories such as regulation of transcription, signal transduction, response to various stress conditions, cell wall biogenesis and plant hormone-related functions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 4</a>). It is reasonable to expect that biological systems involved in these categories contributed to primary terrestrial adaptation. These analyses suggested that an ancestor of <i>K. flaccidum</i> had already acquired genes crucial for terrestrial life. In particular, plant hormone-mediated signal transduction pathways were likely essential for the evolution of responses to environmental stimuli in land plants.</p><p>Many plant hormones have also been detected in both unicellular and multicellular algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Tarakhovskaya, E. R., Maslov, Y. I. &amp; Shishova, M. F. Phytohormones in algae. Russ. J. Plant Physiol. 54, 163â170 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1550">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Le Bail, A. et al. Auxin metabolism and function in the multicellular brown alga Ectocarpus siliculosus. Plant Physiol. 153, 128â144 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1553">15</a></sup>, but their functions in algae remain mostly unclear. Analysis of the <i>K. flaccidum</i> genome revealed candidates for most of the genes required for the biosynthesis of auxin, abscisic acid (ABA), and jasmonic acid (JA) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 5</a>). Moreover, detection of plant hormones with mass spectrometry unambiguously indicated the presence in <i>K. flaccidum</i> of the auxin indole-3-acetic acid, ABA, the cytokinin isopentenyladenine, JA, and salicylic acid (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 6</a>). In addition, we identified genes predicted to encode counterparts of the plant hormone receptors <i>ABP1</i> (auxin), <i>GTG</i> (ABA), <i>CRE1</i> (cytokinin) and <i>ETR</i> (ethylene) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig5">Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Overview of predicted plant hormone signalling in K. flaccidum."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Overview of predicted plant hormone signalling in <i>K. flaccidum.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="289" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Plant hormones were quantified by mass spectrometry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Boxes highlighted in light blue, yellow, and surrounded by broken lines represent detected, unmeasured, and undetectable plant hormones, respectively. Green ellipses represent putative counterparts, and dashed ellipses represent undetected counterparts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 5</a>). Receptors for which putative genes were found in the <i>K. flaccidum</i> genome are indicated against a light-blue background.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also compared organellar genes found in other algae and land plants. A notable feature of the <i>K. flaccidum</i> plastid genome was the presence of 18 NADH oxidoreductase subunits that constitute the NADH dehydrogenase-like complex (NDH) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 6 and 7</a>), which mediates CEF in photosystemI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Niyogi, K. K. Photoprotection revisited: genetic and molecular approaches. Annu. Rev. Plant. Physiol. Plant. Mol. Biol. 50, 333â359 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1633">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="MÃ¼ller, P., Li, X. &amp; Niyogi, K. K. Non-photochemical quenching. a response to excess light energy. Plant Physiol. 125, 1558â1566 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1636">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ifuku, K., Endo, T., Shikanai, T. &amp; Aro, E.-M. Structure of the chloroplast NADH dehydrogenase-like complex: nomenclature for nuclear-encoded subunits. Plant Cell Physiol. 52, 1560â1568 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1639">18</a></sup>. Several stresses, including high-intensity light and drought, can activate CEF. It is believed that CEF increases the proton gradient across the thylakoid membrane, which induces non-photochemical quenching (NPQ) and ATP synthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Niyogi, K. K. Photoprotection revisited: genetic and molecular approaches. Annu. Rev. Plant. Physiol. Plant. Mol. Biol. 50, 333â359 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1643">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rumeau, D., Peltier, G. &amp; Cournac, L. Chlororespiration and cyclic electron flow around PSI during photosynthesis and plant stress response. Plant Cell Environ. 30, 1041â1051 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1646">19</a></sup>. These responses dissipate excess light energy and enable various adaptive responses to stress. Land plants have two CEF pathways, namely the PGR5 and NDH pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rumeau, D., Peltier, G. &amp; Cournac, L. Chlororespiration and cyclic electron flow around PSI during photosynthesis and plant stress response. Plant Cell Environ. 30, 1041â1051 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1651">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Munekage, Y. et al. PGR5 is involved in cyclic electron flow around photosystem I and is essential for photoprotection in Arabidopsis. Cell 110, 361â371 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1654">20</a></sup>, but no genes encoding NDH have been found in algae except for members of Charophyta and some Prasinophyceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="MartÃ­n, M. &amp; Sabater, B. Plastid ndh genes in plant evolution. Plant Physiol. Biochem. 48, 636â645 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1658">21</a></sup>. Here we identified seven genes in the <i>K. flaccidum</i> nuclear genome that encode NDH components and <i>PGR5</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 7</a>). Although some NDH genes were not identified, the <i>K. flaccidum</i> genome harbours genes that encode major NDH components (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 7</a>). A CEF activity mediated by the NDH pathway has been detected as a transient increase in chlorophyll fluorescence after turning off actinic light by pulse-amplitude-modulated fluorometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Shikanai, T. et al. Directed disruption of the tobacco ndhB gene impairs cyclic electron flow around photosystem I. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 95, 9705â9709 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1681">22</a></sup>. Our analysis clearly demonstrated that <i>K. flaccidum</i> has the CEF activity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig7">Fig. 7a,b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Predicted NDH complex and related genes in K. flaccidum."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Predicted NDH complex and related genes in <i>K. flaccidum.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="230" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Green boxes indicate that putative counterparts identified, and open boxes surrounded by broken lines indicate that no putative counterparts were found (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 7</a>). Genes with names written in blue reside within the chloroplast genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Measurement of cyclic electron transport."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7: Measurement of cyclic electron transport.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig7_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms4978/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms4978_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1425" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Transient increases in chlorophyll fluorescence after <i>K. flaccidum</i> was kept in the dark (<b>a</b>) or exposed to far-red light (FR, &gt;740ânm, <b>b</b>). Each insert indicates the transient increase in chlorophyll fluorescence after 2âmin of illumination with actinic light (AL, 150âÎ¼molâm<sup>â2</sup>âs<sup>â1</sup>). The transient increase of chlorophyll fluorescence in darkness after exposure to actinic light was quenched by subsequent exposure to FR light. These data demonstrate the existence of cyclic electron flow through the NDH pathway.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms4978/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>We showed <i>K. flaccidum</i> produced several plant hormones. Moreover, we found some counterparts for key components in the hormone signalling pathways are encoded in the genome. Of special interest is the likely importance of ABA as a key factor for terrestrialization, because ABA is a central signalling molecule needed to adapt to abiotic stresses such as drought, salinity and freezing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Hauser, F., Waadt, R. &amp; Schroeder, J. I. Evolution of abscisic acid synthesis and signaling mechanisms. Curr. Biol. 21, R346âR355 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1763">23</a></sup>. Although we identified counterparts of the hormone receptors <i>ABP1</i>, <i>GTG</i>, <i>CRE1</i> and <i>ETR</i> for auxin, ABA, cytokinin and ethylene respectively, we did not detect putative genes for other known receptors, such as <i>TIRs</i> (auxin), <i>PYR/PYL/RCAR</i> (ABA), <i>GID</i> (gibberellin), <i>COI1</i> (JA-isoleucine) and NPR (salicylic acid) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig5">Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Among them, the TIRs, GID and COI1 are coupled with protein turnover mediated by the ubiquitinâproteasome system and enable crosstalk among plant hormone signalling pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Santner, A. &amp; Estelle, M. Recent advances and emerging trends in plant hormone signalling. Nature 459, 1071â1078 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1799">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Robert-Seilaniantz, A., Grant, M. &amp; Jones, J. D. Hormone crosstalk in plant disease and defense: more than just jasmonate-salicylate antagonism. Annu Rev. Phytopathol. 49, 317â343 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1802">25</a></sup>. It is thus interesting that most of the plant hormone signalling machineries that are dependent on SCF (Skp, Cullin and F-box-containing protein) complexes are probably missing in <i>K. flaccidum</i>, although <i>K. flaccidum</i> encodes putative variants of functional receptors and transporters found in land plants, such as <i>ABP1</i>, <i>PIN</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Viaene, T., Delwiche, C. F., Rensing, S. A. &amp; Friml, J. Origin and evolution of PIN auxin transporters in the green lineage. Trends Plant Sci. 18, 5â10 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1818">26</a></sup> and <i>AUX</i>, which are involved in auxin sensing and transport. PINs transport auxin between plants cells and thus have crucial roles in many developmental processes. Arabidopsis produces a novel type of PINs with a short hydrophilic loop in the central region, and these PINs localize to the endoplasmic reticulum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Viaene, T., Delwiche, C. F., Rensing, S. A. &amp; Friml, J. Origin and evolution of PIN auxin transporters in the green lineage. Trends Plant Sci. 18, 5â10 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1825">26</a></sup>. <i>KfPIN</i> was intermediate in size between short- and long-type <i>PINs</i> in our gene models (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 5 and 6</a>). Further analysis will reveal whether KfPIN directly facilitates auxin transport between cells.</p><p>Genomic evidence suggests that <i>K. flaccidum</i> has certain types of primitive land-plant signalling pathways for plant hormone responses. The primitive plant hormone responses like those found in <i>K. flaccidum</i> may have further evolved in land plants by coupling with more refined signalling networks such as those involving ubiquitin-mediated proteolysis. These primitive hormone signallings in <i>K. flaccidum</i> may facilitate various responses of this alga to harsh environmental stresses on land. In addition, these hormone systems may play important roles in cellâcell communication in this organism. We tried to find some gene families specific in multicellular organisms (<i>Clathrus crispus</i>, <i>Ectocarpus siliculosus</i>, <i>Volvox carteri</i>, <i>K. flaccidum</i> and land plants). However, we did not detect any increase in the number of genes that are characteristic of multicellular organisms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). In these organisms, multicellularity has evolved independently, and thus comparison between unicellular and multicellular charophytic algae will be necessary to clarify the multicellularity of land plants similarly to study of Volvox<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223â226 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1866">27</a></sup>. However, genes related to multicellularity (<i>WUSCHEL</i>, <i>AGAMOUS</i> like MADS-box gene in land plants, <i>GNOM</i>, and several cell wall-related genes) exist in <i>K. flaccidum</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 5</a>). These results suggest that the ancestor of <i>K. flaccidum</i> probably had made a start toward organizing the current complex multicellular systems while it still had a simple body plan.</p><p>We showed CEF activity in Photosystem I in this alga. Two different inducers of NPQâPsbS and the Lhc-like polypeptide LHCSRâare known in algae and land plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 7</a>). In land plants, NPQ relies mainly on PSBS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li, X. P. et al. A pigment-binding protein essential for regulation of photosynthetic light harvesting. Nature 403, 391â395 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1895">28</a></sup>, whereas in green algae NPQ relies mainly on LHCSR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Peers, G. et al. An ancient light-harvesting protein is critical for the regulation of algal photosynthesis. Nature 462, 518â521 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1899">29</a></sup>. PSBS and LHCSR work independently through different mechanisms. In <i>P. patens</i>, PSBS and LHCSR act additively to induce strong NPQ for efficient photoprotection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Gerotto, C. &amp; Morosinotto, T. Evolution of photoprotection mechanisms upon land colonization: evidence of PSBS-dependent NPQ in late Streptophyte algae. Physiol. Plant 149, 583â598 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1906">30</a></sup>. In this regard, <i>K. flaccidum</i> likely relies on LHCSR, whereas PSBS function predominates in the late-diverging charophyta (<i>Zygnematales</i>, <i>Coleochetales</i> and <i>Charales</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Gerotto, C. &amp; Morosinotto, T. Evolution of photoprotection mechanisms upon land colonization: evidence of PSBS-dependent NPQ in late Streptophyte algae. Physiol. Plant 149, 583â598 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17367e1923">30</a></sup>. Although we detected <i>psbS</i> mRNA in <i>K. flaccidum</i>, further work is necessary to clarify the role of PSBS in this alga.</p><p>Our genome analysis of <i>K. flaccidum</i> reveals the presence and functionality of several important stress responses found in terrestrial plants. Although the protein sets encoded by these genes are primitive, they may be sufficient to guide a primitive body plan and direct the tissue differentiation needed to define a terrestrial alga. Future research on each genomic factor in this organism and further analyses of other charophyte genomes may assist our understanding of the events that enabled plants to colonize land.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genome sequencing and annotation</h3><p>Genomic DNA and expressed mRNAs of <i>K. flaccidum</i> strain NIES-2285 were extracted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Methods</a>) and sequenced using the Roche 454 GS FLX Titanium and Illumina GAIIx platforms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Methods</a>). A total of 5.4âGb (genomic DNA) and 570âMb (transcriptome) were assembled using Newbler (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Methods</a>). Chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes were assembled independently of the nuclear genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Methods</a>). Sequencing and assembly of the nuclear genome was validated using bowtie2, SPALN, BLAST and MEGAN (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Methods</a>). Organellar genes were predicted and annotated using Glimmer3, GeneMarkP, GeneMark (a heuristic approach for gene prediction), FGENESB, tRNAScan-SE, RNAmmer and BLAST with additional manual curation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Methods</a>). Assembled transcript sequences were mapped to scaffolds using SPALN. Nuclear genes were modelled and predicted by Augustus. These genes were annotated with blast2GO, BLASTP, interpro, Gclust, targetP, ipsort, KAAS, clustalW, MUSCLE, Gblocks and FastTree with additional manual curation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Methods</a>). The assembled scaffolds sequences have been deposited at DDBJ. The data also can be freely accessed through the projectâs website <a href="http://www.plantmorphogenesis.bio.titech.ac.jp/~algae_genome_project/klebsormidium/index.html">http://www.plantmorphogenesis.bio.titech.ac.jp/~algae_genome_project/klebsormidium/index.html</a>. A basic BLAST tool to search nucleotide and protein databases is accessible at <a href="http://genome.microbedb.jp/klebsormidium">http://genome.microbedb.jp/klebsormidium</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Species used for comparative genome analyses</h3><p><i>K. flaccidum</i> genes were compared with those of nine other algae (<i>Chondrus crispus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Collen, J. et al. USA. 110, 5247â5252 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2003">31</a></sup>, <i>Ectocarpus siliculosus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Cock, J. M. et al. The Ectocarpus genome and the independent evolution of multicellularity in brown algae. Science 318, 245â250 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2055">38</a></sup>), eight charophyte ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Timme, R. E., Bachvaroff, T. R. &amp; Delwiche, C. F. Broad phylogenomic sampling and the sister lineage of land plants.
