Patens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Rensing, S. A. et al. The Physcomitrella Genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science 319, 64â69 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2092">6</a></sup>, <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. Science 332, 960â963 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2098">39</a></sup>, <i>Oryza sativa</i> subsp. Japonica<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="International rice genome sequencing project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2106">40</a></sup>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2112">41</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Arabidopsis genome initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2118">42</a></sup>). Gene data in JGI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Grigoriev, I. V. et al. The genome portal of the Department of Energy Joint Genome Institute. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D26âD32 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2122">43</a></sup>, Phytozome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178âD1186 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2127">44</a></sup> or the RefSeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Pruitt, K. D., Tatusova, T., Brown, G. R. &amp; Maglott, D. R. NCBI Reference Sequences (RefSeq): current status, new features and genome annotation policy. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D130âD135 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2131">45</a></sup> release version 54 data set were used for all species except for three algal speciesâ<i>C. merolae</i>, <i>E. siliculosus</i> and <i>C. crispus</i>. These data were used as two data sets: Data set 1 (mainly JGI data) and Data set 2 (mainly refseq data) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Each data set yielded the same conclusion (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Tables 2â5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig3">Figs 3a,b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig4">4aâc</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 3 and 8â12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Classification of genes</h3><p>All-against-all BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 215, 403â410 (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2168">46</a></sup> analysis was applied to all genes of the 15 species analysed (<i>e</i>-value &lt;1eâ3, no filter query sequence). The proteins of each species that were reciprocally assigned the highest scores relative to the genes of the other species were then extracted. Only the proteins of each species for which alignments covered &gt;50% of the query and database sequences were used for this analysis. After extracting the proteins with reciprocal best hits, homologous clusters were identified by clustering analysis using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Li, L., Christian, J. S. &amp; David, S. R. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2175">47</a></sup> with following parameters: inflation value=1.5, percentMatchCutoff=1 and evalueExponentCutoff=â3. These homologous clusters were classified into four categories: (1) clusters found only algae, (2) clusters found only in land plants, (3) clusters found in both algae and land plants and (4) no reciprocal best hit to other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). For this analysis, <i>K. flaccidum</i> was not considered as the reference for both algae and land plants.</p><p>We also classified homologous clusters into four categories: (1) clusters found only in unicellular organisms, (2) clusters found only in multicellular organisms, (3) clusters found in both unicellular and multicellular organisms and (4) no reciprocal best hit to other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Heat maps for gene classification</h3><p>First, homologous groups produced by OrthoMCL that contained <i>K. flaccidum</i> genes were selected. As a result, 5,447 gene groups were extracted as non-unique groups shared by <i>K. flaccidum</i> and other organisms and used for subsequent analysis. Against each group, the presence or absence of genes in individual organisms was checked. Then, Pearsonâs correlation coefficient between each gene was calculated as a distance matrix, and a gene cluster was constructed using the complete linkage method. Finally, a binary heat map profile with a dendrogram was created (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). All statistical analyses were performed with the R programme version 2.15.1 ( <a href="http://www.r-project.org/">http://www.r-project.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Phylogenetic tree with Charophyta species</h3><p>A total of 160 ortholog data sets that contained amino acid sequences of Charophyta were obtained from previous research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Timme, R. E., Bachvaroff, T. R. &amp; Delwiche, C. F. Broad phylogenomic sampling and the sister lineage of land plants. PLoS ONE 7, e29696 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2234">5</a></sup>. Sequences originating from <i>Mesostigma</i> were removed from the above data sets because only a few orthologue groups were contained in its EST sequence. BLASTP (<i>e</i>-value &lt;1eâ3, no filter query sequence) was then applied to our <i>K. flaccidum</i> sequence against <i>K. flaccidum</i> sequences within the above data sets to merge the homologous groups produced by OrthoMCL and corresponding Charophyta ortholog groups. In addition, homologous groups for which each algae species had only one sequence were chosen. As a result, 31 homologous groups were selected and merged as the Charophyta ortholog group (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 1</a>). Each merged group was aligned using MAFFT version 6.934 beta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059â3066 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2254">48</a></sup> with default parameters. Alignments were then concatenated by species. The maximum-likelihood approach was applied to construct a phylogenetic tree using MEGA version 5.05 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731â2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2257">49</a>) with the JTT+F+gamma model. In MEGA5, the partial deletion method with an 80% cut off was chosen to remove ambiguous sites (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Reciprocal BLASTP best-hit analysis</h3><p>Statistical analysis of best reciprocal protein and EST hits for <i>K. flaccidum</i> with other organisms was performed as follows. The number of best reciprocal hits for protein or EST pairs for <i>K. flaccidum</i> (16,063 genes) with five plants proteins, nine algae and other seven charophyte algae ESTs were extracted with a BLASTP or TBLASTN-BLASTX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Li, L., Christian, J. S. &amp; David, S. R. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2278">47</a></sup> reciprocal search (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>BLASTP bit score analysis of the reciprocal best-hit protein for <i>K. flaccidum</i> between nine algae and five land plants was performed as follows.</p><p>A total of 5,495 genes in <i>K. flaccidum</i> had reciprocal BLASTP best-hit pairs with both algae and land plant proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 8</a>). These BLASTP and reciprocal BLASTP bit scores with the best-hit proteins of algae and land plants were plotted on the <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> axes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>).</p><p>TBLASTN-BLASTX reciprocal best-hit numbers of Charophyta ESTs to gene families for which the numbers of genes were significantly increased in land plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 2</a>) was performed as follows. <i>K. flaccidum</i> protein sequences in each group were used as query sequences. The numbers of reciprocal best hits for <i>K. flaccidum</i> genes in each group were extracted by a TBLASTN-BLASTX reciprocal search with nine charophyte algae EST databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>In <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 7</a>, best candidate counterparts in charophyte ESTs for each <i>K. flaccidum</i> gene were estimated by a TBLASTN-BLASTX reciprocal search with nine charophyte algae EST databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Best-hits EST sequences that had sufficient sequence length and an appropriate amino-acid sequence frame for multiple alignment were used to construct a gene phylogenetic tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 13â73</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Gene family analysis</h3><p>For this analysis, paralogues were defined as genes attributed to the homologous group of OrthoMCL that contained at least two genes, and the singletons then became the genes lacking a paralogue for each species. Hence, the paralogues and singletons represented a gene family for each species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 3 and 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Functional estimation of gene families</h3><p>The functions of gene families that belonged to land plants for which the numbers of genes were significantly larger than those of algae (median of land plant gene numbers/median of algal gene numbers â¥10; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>) were estimated using <i>A. thaliana</i> GOSLIM data of The Arabidopsis Information Resource ftp site ( <a href="ftp://ftp.arabidopsis.org/home/tair/Ontologies/Gene_Ontology/">ftp://ftp.arabidopsis.org/home/tair/Ontologies/Gene_Ontology/</a>). The number of genes in each gene ontology category for <i>A. thaliana</i> proteins in each group was counted, and the top three categories of molecular functions and biological processes are noted in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 2</a>. The numbers of genes and groups in each gene ontology biological process category are noted in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 3</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Analysis of domains and domain combinations</h3><p>The protein domains of each species were searched with PfamScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Finn, R. D. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, D211âD222 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2402">12</a></sup> using the -pfamB option and Pfam27.0 database. PF13352, PB019699 and PB009748, which are specific to <i>P. patens</i> and highly repetitive, were removed from the analysis. The domains and domain combinations were counted using Perl scripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Tables 4 and 5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig4">Fig. 4b,c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 11 and 12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Functional estimation of Streptophyta-specific genes</h3><p>The 865 <i>A. thaliana</i> counterparts of 1,238 Streptophyta-specific genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>) in <i>K. flaccidum</i> were predicted by BLASTP best hits with the criterion that each best-hit gene be in the same gene family between these two species. The numbers of genes and groups of <i>K. flaccidum</i> for which their <i>Arabidopsis</i> counterparts are found in each gene ontology biological process category were counted using <i>A. thaliana</i> GOSLIM with Perl scripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Phylogenetic tree</h3><p>Protein and EST sequences were collected from data set 1 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 7</a>) and charophyte ESTs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 8</a>) by BLASTP and BLASTX for phylogenetic analysis of all proteins shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig5">Figs 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig6">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 5 and 7</a>. After removing insufficient sequences for phylogenetic analysis (short sequence length, low quality, large deletion, and so on), sequences were aligned with MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2473">50</a></sup>. Gblocks 0.91b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Talavera, G. &amp; Castresana, J. Improvement of phylogenies after removing divergent and ambiguously aligned blocks from protein sequence alignments. Syst. Biol. 56, 564â577 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2477">51</a></sup> was used to remove any poorly conserved regions, and the amino acid substitution model was calculated by Aminosan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Tanabe, A. S. Kakusan4 and Aminosan: two programs for comparing nonpartitioned, proportional, and separate models for combined molecular phylogenetic analyses of multilocus sequence data. Mol. Ecol. Res. 11, 914â921 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2481">52</a></sup>. Phylogenetic analyses were performed in MEGA-CC ver 5.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Peterson, D. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA-CC: computing core of molecular evolutionary genetics analysis program for automated and iterative data analysis. Bioinformatics 28, 2685â2686 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2484">53</a>) with 500 bootstraps. Bootstrap values higher than 50 are indicated under each branch (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 13â73</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Genes involved in plant hormone biosynthesis and signalling</h3><p>Candidate counterparts in <i>K. flaccidum</i> were estimated by BLAST and phylogenetic analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 5</a>). <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 5</a> also includes information of candidates counterparts in other species. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig5">Figure 5</a> is based on a previous study and reviews<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Niyogi, K. K. Photoprotection revisited: genetic and molecular approaches. Annu. Rev. Plant. Physiol. Plant. Mol. Biol. 50, 333â359 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2511">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="MÃ¼ller, P., Li, X. &amp; Niyogi, K. K. Non-photochemical quenching. a response to excess light energy. Plant Physiol. 125, 1558â1566 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2514">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Santner, A., Calderon-Villalobos, L. I. &amp; Estelle, M. Plant hormones are versatile chemical regulators of plant growth. Nat. Chem. Biol. 5, 301â307 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2517">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Shi, J. H. &amp; Yang, Z. B. Is ABP1 an auxin receptor yet? Mol. Plant 4, 635â640 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2520">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Fu, Z. Q. et al. NPR3 and NPR4 are receptors for the immune signal salicylic acid in plants. Nature 486, 228â232 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2523">56</a></sup>. Multiple alignment, membrane spanning region and hydrophobicity profile of amino acid sequences of PINs were calculated and drawn by MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2528">50</a></sup>, BioEdit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Hall, T. A. BioEdit: a user-friendly biological sequence alignment editor and analysis program for Windows 95/98/NT. Nucl. Acids. Symp. Ser 41, 95â98 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2532">57</a></sup>, Tmpred<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Hofmann, K. &amp; Stoffel, W. TMbaseâa database of membrane spanning proteins segments. Biol. Chem. Hoppe Seyler 374, 166 (1993)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2536">58</a></sup> and Kyte-Doolittle scale<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kyte, J. &amp; Doolittle, R. F. A simple method for displaying the hydropathic character of a protien. J. Mol. Biol. 157, 105â132 (1982)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2540">59</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 5 and 6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Plant hormone quantification</h3><p><i>K. flaccidum</i> cells were statically cultured for 5 days in fresh liquid C medium under continuous light (10âÎ¼mol photons m<sup>â2</sup>âs<sup>â1</sup>). Plant hormones were extracted as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yoshimoto, K. et al. Autophagy negatively regulates cell death by controlling NPR1-dependent salicylic acid signaling during senescence and the innate immune response in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 21, 2914â2927 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2561">60</a></sup> with modifications, as follows. Lyophilized samples (~150âmg) were placed in 14-ml round-bottom tubes and ground into powder with 10-mm ceramic beads and liquid nitrogen with vortexing. The ground samples were extracted with 5âml of 80% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetonitrile containing 1% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetic acid for 1âh with internal standards (<sup>13</sup>C<sub>6</sub>-JA-isoleucine, d<sub>2</sub>-JA, d<sub>6</sub>-SA, d<sub>6</sub>-ABA, d<sub>2</sub>-IAA, d<sub>2</sub>-GA<sub>1</sub>, d<sub>2</sub>-GA<sub>4</sub>, d<sub>5</sub>-tZ, d<sub>3</sub>-DHZ and d<sub>6</sub>-iP). The supernatants were collected after centrifugation at 1,663â<i>g</i> for 20âmin, and the pellets were extracted again with 5âml of 80% acetonitrile containing 1% acetic acid. The supernatants were collected after centrifugation at 1,663â<i>g</i> for 20âmin, and the combined supernatants were further purified for hormone analysis. After removing acetonitrile in the supernatants, the acidic water extracts were loaded onto Oasis HLB cartridge columns (500âmg, 6âml, Waters, Milford, MA, USA) and washed with 6âml of water containing 1% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetic acid to remove highly polar impurities. Fractions containing plant hormones were then eluted with 12âml of 80% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetonitrile containing 1% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetic acid. After removing acetonitrile in the eluate via vacuum centrifugation, the acidic water extracts were loaded onto Oasis MCX cartridge columns (30âmg, 1âml, Waters). After washing the columns with 1âml of water containing 1% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetic acid, acidic and neutral compounds (AN fractions) were eluted with 2âml of 80% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetonitrile containing 1% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetic acid. Ten per cent of each AN fraction was used for SA analysis. After washing the Oasis MCX columns with 1âml of water containing 5% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) ammonia, basic compounds containing tZ, DHZ and iP were eluted with 2âml of 60% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetonitrile containing 5% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) ammonia. After removing acetonitrile in the remaining 90% of the AN fractions, acidic water extracts were loaded onto Oasis WAX cartridge columns (30âmg, 1âml, Waters). After washing the columns with 1âml of water containing 1% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetic acid, neutral compounds were eluted with 2âml of 80% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetonitrile and fractions containing acidic compounds (IAA, ABA, JA, JA-isoleucine, GA<sub>1</sub> and GA<sub>4</sub>) were collected with 2âml of 80% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetonitrile containing 1% (<i>v</i>/<i>v</i>) acetic acid. Hormones were quantified with liquid chromatography-coupled electrospray ionizationâtandem mass spectrometry. The LC gradient condition of ABA, GA<sub>1</sub>, GA<sub>4</sub>,IAA, JA and JA-Ile was as follows: Solvent A (water containing 0.01% acetic acid), Solvent B (acetonitrile, 0.05% acetic acid) The gradients were programmed for changes of 3â50% composition of solvent B over 15âmin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yoshimoto, K. et al. Autophagy negatively regulates cell death by controlling NPR1-dependent salicylic acid signaling during senescence and the innate immune response in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 21, 2914â2927 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2703">60</a></sup>. The LC gradient condition of SA was as follows: Solvent A (water containing 0.1% formic acid) and Solvent B (acetonitrile, 0.1% formic acid). The gradients were programmed for changes of 3â98% composition of solvent B over 10âmin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yoshimoto, K. et al. Autophagy negatively regulates cell death by controlling NPR1-dependent salicylic acid signaling during senescence and the innate immune response in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 21, 2914â2927 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2708">60</a></sup>. The LC gradient condition of tZ, DHZ and iP was as follows: Solvent A (water containing 0.01% acetic acid) and Solvent B (acetonitrile, 0.05% acetic acid) The gradients were programmed for changes of 3â22% composition of solvent B over 27âmin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Yoshimoto, K. et al. Autophagy negatively regulates cell death by controlling NPR1-dependent salicylic acid signaling during senescence and the innate immune response in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 21, 2914â2927 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2712">60</a></sup>. Detected plant hormones were summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Table 6</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Genes involved in cyclic electron transport</h3><p>Ndh genes in chloroplast genomes of 198 species were listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 6</a>. Candidate counterparts in <i>K. flaccidum</i> were estimated by BLAST and phylogenetic analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 7</a>). <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Data 7</a> also includes information of candidate counterparts in other species. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig6">Figure 6</a> is based on the composition of the NDH complex determined for land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Shikanai, T. et al. Directed disruption of the tobacco ndhB gene impairs cyclic electron flow around photosystem I. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 95, 9705â9709 (1998)." href="/articles/ncomms4978#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17367e2743">22</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Measurement of cyclic electron transport</h3><p>Cells of <i>K. flaccidum</i> were spotted onto a Protran nitrocellulose membrane (Whatman, Dassel, Germany) by vacuum filtration and adapted to darkness by incubation in the dark for 5âmin. CEF of the spotted cells was monitored by MINI-PAM (Waltz, Effeltrich, Germany). Cells were exposed to actinic light (150âÎ¼molâm<sup>â2</sup>âs<sup>â1</sup>) for 2âmin. Far-red light was generated by filtering halogen light through a Fuji SC74 filter (&gt;740ânm). The transient increase of chlorophyll fluorescence in the presence or absence of far-red light was then compared (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#Fig7">Fig. 7a,b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Other analysis</h3><p>Methods for organellar genomes assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 74</a>), nuclear genome validation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 75â77</a>), organellar genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Fig. 78</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Tables 10 and 11</a>), transposable elements prediction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Tables 1 and 12</a>), non-coding RNAs prediction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Tables 1 and 12</a>) and genome duplication (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Figs 79 and 80</a>) are described in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms4978#MOESM652">Supplementary Methods</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec24"
